<p><b>Vienna</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/En-ca-Vienna.oga" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" data-file-height="11" data-file-width="11" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Vienna.ogg" title="File:En-us-Vienna.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wien</i></span>, <small>pronounced</small> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_German" title="Help:IPA for German">[viːn]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:Wien1.ogg" title="File:Wien1.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/Wien1.ogg" title="Wien1.ogg">listen</a>)</small>) is the capital and largest city of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and one of the nine <a href="/wiki/States_of_Austria" title="States of Austria">states of Austria</a>. Vienna is Austria's <a href="/wiki/Primate_city" title="Primate city">primary city</a>, with a population of about 1.8 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistik.at_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-statistik.at-1">[1]</a></sup> (2.6 million within the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nahverkehrsoffensive1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-nahverkehrsoffensive1-4">[4]</a></sup> nearly one third of Austria's population), and its <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Austria" title="Culture of Austria">cultural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Austria" title="Economy of Austria">economic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Austria" title="Politics of Austria">political</a> centre. It is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Largest_cities_of_the_European_Union_by_population_within_city_limits" title="Largest cities of the European Union by population within city limits">7th-largest city</a> by population within city limits in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. Until the beginning of the 20th century, it was the largest <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-speaking</a> city in the world, and before the splitting of the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the city had 2 million inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Today, it has the second largest number of German speakers after <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Population11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Population11-12">[12]</a></sup> Vienna is host to many major <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">international organizations</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>. The city is located in the eastern part of Austria and is close to the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>. These regions work together in a European <a href="/wiki/Centrope" title="Centrope">Centrope</a> border region. Along with nearby <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>, Vienna forms a metropolitan region with 3 million inhabitants. In 2001, the city centre was designated a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apart from being regarded as the <i>City of Music</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> because of its musical legacy, Vienna is also said to be "The City of Dreams" because it was home to the world's first psycho-analyst – <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The city's roots lie in early <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> settlements that transformed into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">Medieval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> city, and then the capital of the Austro-Hungarian Empire. It is well known for having played an essential role as a leading European music centre, from the great age of Viennese Classicism through the early part of the 20th century. The historic centre of Vienna is rich in architectural ensembles, including Baroque castles and gardens, and the late-19th-century Ringstraße lined with grand buildings, monuments and parks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vienna is known for its high quality of life. In a 2005 study of 127 <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">world cities</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a> ranked the city first (in a tie with <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver, Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco, USA</a>) for the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_most_liveable_cities" title="World's most liveable cities">world's most liveable cities</a>. Between 2011 and 2015, Vienna was ranked second, behind <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne, Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> For eight consecutive years (2009–2016), the human-resource-consulting firm <a href="/wiki/Mercer_(consulting_firm)" title="Mercer (consulting firm)">Mercer</a> ranked Vienna first in its annual <a href="/wiki/Mercer_Quality_of_Living_Survey" title="Mercer Quality of Living Survey">"Quality of Living" survey</a> of hundreds of cities around the world, a title the city still held in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Monocle's 2015 "Quality of Life Survey" ranked Vienna second on a list of the top 25 cities in the world "to make a base within."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Settlements_Programme" title="United Nations Human Settlements Programme">UN-Habitat</a> has classified Vienna as being the most prosperous city in the world in 2012/2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The city was ranked 1st globally for its culture of innovation in 2007 and 2008, and sixth globally (out of 256 cities) in the 2014 Innovation Cities Index, which analyzed 162 indicators in covering three areas: culture, infrastructure, and markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Vienna regularly hosts <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> conferences and is often used as a case study by urban planners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 2005 and 2010, Vienna was the world's number-one destination for international congresses and conventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It attracts over 3.7 million tourists a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Names_of_European_cities_in_different_languages:_U-Z#V" title="Names of European cities in different languages: U-Z">Other names of Vienna</a></div>
<p>The English name <i>Vienna</i> is borrowed from the homonymous Italian version of the city's name or the French <i>Vienne</i>. The etymology of the city's name is still subject to scholarly dispute. Some claim that the name comes from <i>Vedunia</i>, meaning "forest stream", which subsequently produced the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>Uuenia</i> (<i>Wenia</i> in modern writing), the <a href="/wiki/New_High_German" title="New High German">New High German</a> <i>Wien</i> and its dialectal variant <i>Wean</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Others believe that the name comes from the Roman settlement name of Celtic extraction <i><a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a></i>, probably meaning "fair village, white settlement" from the Celtic roots <i>vindo-</i>, meaning "bright" or "fair" – as in the Irish <i>fionn</i> and the Welsh <i>gwyn</i> –, and <i>-bona</i> "village, settlement".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> A variant of this Celtic name could be preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a> names of the city (<i>Vídeň</i> and <i>Viedeň</i> respectively) and in that of the city's district <a href="/wiki/Wieden" title="Wieden">Wieden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name of the city in Hungarian (<i>Bécs</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian_language" title="Serbo-Croatian language">Serbo-Croatian</a> (<i>Beč</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a> (<i>Beç</i>) has a different, probably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavonic</a> origin, and originally referred to an <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avars" title="Pannonian Avars">Avar</a> fort in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a>-speakers call the city <i>Dunaj</i>, which in other <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central European</a> Slavic languages means the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danube_River" title="Danube River">Danube River</a>, on which the city stands.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Vienna" title="History of Vienna">History of Vienna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Vienna" title="Timeline of Vienna">Timeline of Vienna</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="2072" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png/220px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png/330px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png/440px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_098v99r_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of Vienna in the <i><a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a></i>, 1493</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wien-1683(1686)-Allen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2883" data-file-width="5943" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg/220px-Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg/330px-Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg/440px-Wien-1683%281686%29-Allen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wien-1683(1686)-Allen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1683 Allen (printed 1686)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Evidence has been found of continuous habitation since 500 BC, when the site of Vienna on the Danube River was settled by the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a>. In 15 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> fortified the frontier city they called <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a> to guard the empire against <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic tribes</a> to the north. One of the earliest references to Vienna is from the Jewish historian, <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, who recounts that the king of <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herod_Archelaus" title="Herod Archelaus">Herod Archelaus</a> (ca. 23 BCE – 18 CE) was banished to the city of Vienna in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> by Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Close ties with other Celtic peoples continued through the ages. The Irish monk <a href="/wiki/Coloman_of_Stockerau" title="Coloman of Stockerau">Saint Colman</a> (or Koloman, Irish <i>Colmán</i>, derived from <i>colm</i> "dove") is buried in Melk Abbey and Saint Fergil (<a href="/wiki/Vergilius_of_Salzburg" title="Vergilius of Salzburg">Virgil the Geometer</a>) was Bishop of Salzburg for forty years, and twelfth-century monastic settlements were founded by Irish Benedictines. Evidence of these ties is still evident in Vienna's great <a href="/wiki/Schottenstift" title="Schottenstift">Schottenstift</a> monastery, once home to many Irish monks.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canaletto_(I)_058.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="4702" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg/220px-Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg/330px-Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg/440px-Canaletto_%28I%29_058.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canaletto_(I)_058.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Vienna from Belvedere</i> by <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Bellotto" title="Bernardo Bellotto">Bernardo Bellotto</a>, 1758</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 976, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leopold_I_of_Austria_(Babenberg)" title="Leopold I of Austria (Babenberg)">Leopold I of Babenberg</a> became count of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarian_Ostmark" title="Bavarian Ostmark">Eastern March</a>, a 60-mile district centering on the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> on the eastern frontier of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>. This initial district grew into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rulers_of_Austria" title="Rulers of Austria">duchy of Austria</a>. Each succeeding Babenberg ruler expanded the march east along the Danube eventually encompassing Vienna and the lands immediately east. In 1145, <a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Henry II, Duke of Austria">Duke Henry II Jasomirgott</a> moved the Babenberg family residence from Klosterneuburg to Vienna. Since that time, Vienna remained the center of the Babenberg dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langelbach_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-langelbach-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1440, Vienna became the resident city of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a> dynasty. It eventually grew to become the <i>de facto</i> capital of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> (1483–1806) and a cultural centre for arts and science, music and fine cuisine. <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> occupied the city between 1485–1490.</p>
<p>In the 16th and 17th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> armies were stopped twice outside Vienna (see <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vienna" title="Siege of Vienna">Siege of Vienna</a>, 1529 and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vienna" title="Battle of Vienna">Battle of Vienna</a>, 1683). A <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_Vienna" title="Great Plague of Vienna">plague</a> epidemic ravaged Vienna in 1679, killing nearly a third of its population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Austro-Hungarian_Empire_and_the_early_20th_century">Austro-Hungarian Empire and the early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Austro-Hungarian Empire and the early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="896" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg/220px-Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg/330px-Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg/440px-Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rudolf_von_Alt-Opera_Crossroads_in_Vienna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vienna <i>Ringstraße</i> and State Opera around 1870</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2579" data-file-width="3500" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg/220px-Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg/330px-Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg/440px-Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wien_Burgtheater_um_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Color photo lithograph of Vienna, 1900</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1804, during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Vienna became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a> and continued to play a major role in European and world politics, including hosting the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> in 1814/15. After the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Compromise_of_1867" title="Austro-Hungarian Compromise of 1867">Austro-Hungarian Compromise of 1867</a>, Vienna remained the capital of what was then the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a>. The city was a centre of classical music, for which the title of the <a href="/wiki/First_Viennese_School" title="First Viennese School">First Viennese School</a> is sometimes applied.</p>
<p>During the latter half of the 19th century, the city developed what had previously been the <a href="/wiki/Bastion" title="Bastion">bastions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glacis" title="Glacis">glacis</a> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringstra%C3%9Fe" title="Ringstraße">Ringstraße</a>, a new <a href="/wiki/Boulevard" title="Boulevard">boulevard</a> surrounding the historical town and a major prestige project. Former suburbs were incorporated, and the city of Vienna grew dramatically. In 1918, after World War I, Vienna became capital of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_German-Austria" title="Republic of German-Austria">Republic of German-Austria</a>, and then in 1919 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Republic_of_Austria" title="First Republic of Austria">First Republic of Austria</a>.</p>
<p>From the late 19th century to 1938, the city remained a centre of high culture and <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">modernism</a>. A world capital of music, the city played host to composers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahms" title="Brahms">Brahms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruckner" title="Bruckner">Bruckner</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahler" title="Mahler">Mahler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a>. The city's cultural contributions in the first half of the 20th century included, among many, the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Secession" title="Vienna Secession">Vienna Secession</a> movement, <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Viennese_School" title="Second Viennese School">Second Viennese School</a>, the architecture of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Loos" title="Adolf Loos">Adolf Loos</a> and the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a>. In 1913, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leon_Trotsky" title="Leon Trotsky">Leon Trotsky</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Tito" title="Joseph Tito">Joseph Tito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> all lived within a few miles of each other in central Vienna, some of them being regulars at the same <a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse" title="Coffeehouse">coffeehouses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Within Austria, Vienna was seen as a centre of socialist politics, for which it was sometimes referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Red_Vienna" title="Red Vienna">Red Vienna</a>". The city was a stage to the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Civil_War" title="Austrian Civil War">Austrian Civil War</a> of 1934, when Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Engelbert Dollfuss</a> sent the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Armed_Forces" title="Austrian Armed Forces">Austrian Army</a> to shell civilian housing occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Republikanischer_Schutzbund" title="Republikanischer Schutzbund">socialist militia</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anschluss_and_World_War_II">Anschluss and World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Anschluss and World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></div>
<p>In 1938, after a triumphant entry into Austria, Austrian-born <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> spoke to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_Germans" title="Ethnic Germans">Austrian Germans</a> from the balcony of the Neue Burg, a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hofburg_Imperial_Palace" title="Hofburg Imperial Palace">Hofburg</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Heldenplatz" title="Heldenplatz">Heldenplatz</a>. Between 1938 (after the <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a>) and the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Vienna lost its status as a capital to <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> as Austria ceased to exist and became a part of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. It was not until 1955 that Austria regained full sovereignty.</p>
<p>On 2 April 1945, the Soviets launched the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Offensive" title="Vienna Offensive">Vienna Offensive</a> against the Germans holding the city and besieged it. British and American air raids and artillery duels between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SS" title="SS">SS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> crippled infrastructure, such as tram services and water and power distribution, and destroyed or damaged thousands of public and private buildings. Vienna fell eleven days later. Austria was separated from Germany, and Vienna was restored as the republic's capital city, but the Soviet hold on the city remained until 1955.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Four-power_Vienna">Four-power Vienna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Four-power Vienna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wien_Besatzungszonen.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="1400" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Wien_Besatzungszonen.png/300px-Wien_Besatzungszonen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Wien_Besatzungszonen.png/450px-Wien_Besatzungszonen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Wien_Besatzungszonen.png/600px-Wien_Besatzungszonen.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wien_Besatzungszonen.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Occupation zones in Vienna, 1945–55</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the war, Vienna was part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Austria" title="Occupation of Austria">Soviet-occupied Eastern Austria</a> until September 1945. As in Berlin, Vienna in September 1945 was divided into sectors by the four powers: the US, the UK, France and the Soviet Union and supervised by an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allied_Commission_for_Austria" title="Allied Commission for Austria">Allied Commission</a>. The four-power occupation of Vienna differed in one key respect from that of Berlin: the central area of the city, known as the first district, constituted an international zone in which the four powers alternated control on a monthly basis. The control was policed by the four powers on a <i>de facto</i> day-to-day basis, the famous "four soldiers in a jeep" method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Blockade" title="Berlin Blockade">Berlin Blockade</a> of 1948 raised Western concerns that the Soviets might repeat the blockade in Vienna. The matter was raised in the UK <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What plans have the Government for dealing with a similar situation in Vienna? Vienna is in exactly a similar position to Berlin. It is surrounded by a Soviet Zone of occupation and we have our sector of responsibility in Vienna the same as the Americans and the French. What plans have the Government to deal with a similar situation arising in Vienna in the near future? I hope we shall have an answer, because this is of vital importance. – Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Nutting" title="Anthony Nutting">Anthony Nutting</a>, Honourable Member for <a href="/wiki/Melton_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Melton (UK Parliament constituency)">Melton</a>, 30 June 1948, House of Commons, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>There was a lack of airfields in the Western sectors, and authorities drafted contingency plans to deal with such a blockade. Plans included the laying down of metal landing mats at Schönbrunn. The Soviets did not blockade the city. The <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Agreement" title="Potsdam Agreement">Potsdam Agreement</a> included written rights of land access to the western sectors, whereas no such written guarantees had covered the western sectors of Berlin. During the 10 years of the four-power occupation, Vienna became a hot-bed for international espionage between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_bloc" title="Western bloc">Western</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern blocs</a>. In the wake of the Berlin Blockade, the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> in Vienna took on a different dynamic. While accepting that Germany and Berlin would be divided, the Soviets had decided against allowing the same state of affairs to arise in Austria and Vienna. Here, the Soviet forces controlled districts 2, 4, 10, 20, 21 and 22 and all areas incorporated into Vienna in 1938.</p>
<p>They put up barbed wire fences around the perimeter of <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a> in 1953, but not in Vienna. By 1955, the Soviets, by signing the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_State_Treaty" title="Austrian State Treaty">Austrian State Treaty</a>, agreed to relinquish their occupation zones in Eastern Austria as well as their sector in Vienna. In exchange they required that Austria declare its permanent neutrality after the allied powers had left the country. Thus they ensured that Austria would not be a member of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and that NATO forces would therefore not have direct communications between <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>.</p>
<p>The atmosphere of four-power Vienna is the background for <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a>'s screenplay for the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Third_Man" title="The Third Man">The Third Man</a></i> (1949). Later he adapted the screenplay as a novel and published it. Occupied Vienna is also depicted in the <a href="/wiki/Philip_Kerr" title="Philip Kerr">Philip Kerr</a> novel, <i><a href="/wiki/A_German_Requiem_(novel)" title="A German Requiem (novel)">A German Requiem</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Austrian_State_Treaty_and_afterwards">Austrian State Treaty and afterwards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Austrian State Treaty and afterwards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The four-power control of Vienna lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_State_Treaty" title="Austrian State Treaty">Austrian State Treaty</a> was signed in May 1955. That year, after years of reconstruction and restoration, the State Opera and the Burgtheater, both on the Ringstraße, reopened to the public. The Soviet Union signed the State Treaty only after having been provided with the political guarantee by the federal government to declare Austria's neutrality after the withdrawal of the allied troops. This law of neutrality, passed in late October 1955 (and not the State Treaty itself), ensured that modern Austria would align with neither <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> nor the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_bloc" title="Soviet bloc">Soviet bloc</a>, and is considered one of the reasons for Austria's late entry into the European Union.</p>
<p>In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Austria" title="Chancellor of Austria">Austrian Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Kreisky" title="Bruno Kreisky">Bruno Kreisky</a> inaugurated the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_International_Centre" title="Vienna International Centre">Vienna International Centre</a>, a new area of the city created to host international institutions. Vienna has regained much of its former international stature by hosting international organizations, such as the United Nations (<a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Industrial_Development_Organization" title="United Nations Industrial Development Organization">United Nations Industrial Development Organization</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_at_Vienna" title="United Nations Office at Vienna">United Nations Office at Vienna</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Preparatory_Commission_for_the_Comprehensive_Nuclear-Test-Ban_Treaty_Organization" title="Preparatory Commission for the Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty Organization">Preparatory Commission for the Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td>10,138</td>

<p>Because of the industrialization and migration from other parts of the Empire, the population of Vienna increased sharply during its time as the capital of <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> (1867–1918). In 1910, Vienna had more than two million inhabitants, and was the fourth <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_proper_by_population" title="List of cities proper by population">largest city</a> in Europe after London, Paris and Berlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Around the start of the 20th century, Vienna was the city with the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czech</a> population in the world (after <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> After World War I, many <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czechs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" title="Hungarian people">Hungarians</a> returned to their ancestral countries, resulting in a decline in the Viennese population. After World War II, the Soviets used force to repatriate key workers of Czech, Slovak and Hungarian origins to return to their ethnic homelands to further the Soviet bloc economy.</p>
<p>Under the Nazi regime, 65,000 Jewish people were deported and murdered in concentration camps by Nazi forces; approximately 130,000 fled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2001, 16% of people living in Austria had nationalities other than Austrian, nearly half of whom were from former <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> the next most numerous nationalities in Vienna were <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turks</a> (39,000; 2.5%), <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poles</a> (13,600; 0.9%) and Germans (12,700; 0.8%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-census-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, an official report from Statistics Austria showed that more than 660,000 (38.8%) of the Viennese population have full or partial migrant background, mostly from Ex-Yugoslavia, Turkey, Germany, Poland, Ukraine, Romania and Hungary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statistik.at_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-statistik.at-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wien.gv.at_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wien.gv.at-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2005 to 2015 the city's population grew by 10.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN-Habitat" title="UN-Habitat">UN-Habitat</a>, Vienna could be the fastest growing city out of 17 European metropolitan areas until 2025 with an increase of 4.65% of its population, compared to 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography and climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vienna_20021024.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="639" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Vienna_20021024.JPG/220px-Vienna_20021024.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Vienna_20021024.JPG/330px-Vienna_20021024.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Vienna_20021024.JPG/440px-Vienna_20021024.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vienna_20021024.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite image of Vienna (2002)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Vienna is located in northeastern Austria, at the easternmost extension of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Basin" title="Vienna Basin">Vienna Basin</a>. The earliest settlement, at the location of today's <a href="/wiki/Inner_city" title="Inner city">inner city</a>, was south of the meandering Danube while the city now spans both sides of the river. Elevation ranges from 151 to 542 m (495 to 1,778 ft). The city has a total area of 414.65 square kilometres (160.1 sq mi), making it the largest city in Austria by area.</p>
<p>Vienna lies within a transition of <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> (hovering just below 22 °C in July and August), and features, according to the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen classification</a>, a Cfb (oceanic) -climate. The city has warm summers with average high temperatures of 24 to 33 °C (75 to 91 °F), with maximum exceeding 38 °C (100 °F) and lows of around 17 °C (63 °F). Winters are relatively dry and cold with average temperatures at about freezing point. Spring and autumn are mild. Precipitation is generally moderate throughout the year, averaging 550 mm (21.7 in) annually, with considerable local variations, the Vienna Woods region in the west being the wettest part (700 to 800 mm (28 to 31 in) annually) and the flat plains in the east being the driest part (500 to 550 mm (20 to 22 in) annually). Snow in the winter is not uncommon, but rare compared to Western and Southern regions in Austria.</p>
